<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_276f61f0-ed94-47fa-bc13-7e29800fdbb0'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47534928" />
    <a title="Troy David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1963514-troy-david"><img alt="Troy David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252852416p2/1963514.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47534928?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Troy David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Troy David" href="/user/show/1963514-troy-david">Troy David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1963514-troy-david?shelf=just-another">just-another</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47534928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer339266414591499395">I read a review within the very walls of this site that stated that this novel deserved no more that 2 stars. The reasoning, it seems, is that all of the characters, other than "the crow", are card board and underdeveloped.<br/><br/>I respect people's opinion. And, if I were to judge this book against the greats of literature, or even the greats of Graphic novels, I might have to agree. The story is violently stupid, and stupidly violent. The characters for the most part are underdeveloped stereotypes. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText339266414591499395" style="display:none">I read a review within the very walls of this site that stated that this novel deserved no more that 2 stars. The reasoning, it seems, is that all of the characters, other than "the crow", are card board and underdeveloped.<br /><br />I respect people's opinion. And, if I were to judge this book against the greats of literature, or even the greats of Graphic novels, I might have to agree. The story is violently stupid, and stupidly violent. The characters for the most part are underdeveloped stereotypes. This certainly is not Shakespear. Or, Hawthorne. Or,(fill-in-the-blank).<br /><br />But all art, in my opinion, must first be judged against itself. This story, told in graphic novel format, is not an attempt at classical literature. It is barely even a story. It is revenge. It is heart-ache. This story is a thesis on one man's descent into grief. It is a pictorial study of loss and the inability to see the good when so consumed by agony that one cannot fathom ever experiencing joy again. All of us have felt this reckless torment, where everyday the sun refused to shine, and most of us have clawed our way back to normalcy, replacing grief with acceptance and have moved on. We forget that pain. That pain is too woeful. This story is a reminder of that painful depth. It is a black mirror to our sunny dispositions.<br /><br />In that, this story, presented in graphic novel format, accomplishes exactly what was intended. Graphic novels are not written to be cool. They are written to be honest, just like all other forms of art.<br /><br />T.</span>
  <a data-text-id="339266414591499395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1959277919" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1959277919" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/800291-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1295167791p2/800291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1959277919?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/800291-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857687951" href="/book/show/12452215-the-crow">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1959277919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3891954877375992842">This has been an interesting trip for me. <br/><br/>This book brings back some amazing memories for me - ironic really as its creation was part of the healing process of terribly painful memories for the author.<br/><br/>I will tell my story since James O'Barr's story is very much documented (and even expanded upon in the afterwards in this definitive version - yes me and my afterwards).<br/><br/>I was at uni and one of my best friends has a serious fixation on this book, ironically it was shortly before the film came out (a</span>
  <span id="freeText3891954877375992842" style="display:none">This has been an interesting trip for me. <br /><br />This book brings back some amazing memories for me - ironic really as its creation was part of the healing process of terribly painful memories for the author.<br /><br />I will tell my story since James O'Barr's story is very much documented (and even expanded upon in the afterwards in this definitive version - yes me and my afterwards).<br /><br />I was at uni and one of my best friends has a serious fixation on this book, ironically it was shortly before the film came out (and all the controversy surrounding it). I remember being reluctantly allowed to read the book - he had spend an obscene amount to get one of the original run editions and treasured it above all else. I remember being instructed how to read it and look after it, ironic really considering the number of books even back then I owned and cared for.<br /><br />Well the book is impressive - for its violence, for its anger and energy and for its tender pain. I think its true power really comes from know the story behind it too, that it is not some exploitation but more an exorcism. <br /><br />This book is not for everyone and I am sure many have dismissed it as simply a catalogue of anger and bloody violence but it is a classic and as such went on to influence many more books that followed. I am glad I have found a copy again although I am still processing the idea that there is more nostalgia than anything else, I will have to get back to you on that one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3891954877375992842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_198639767" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/198639767" />
    <a title="Randy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1642575-randy"><img alt="Randy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286656779p2/1642575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/198639767?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Randy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Randy" href="/user/show/1642575-randy">Randy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642575-randy?shelf=comics-graphic-novels">comics-graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642575-randy?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642575-randy?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer198639767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5390686309664810156">Though I saw (and loved) the film the year it came out, I had never read the original graphic novel until a few days ago, after buying it at the Borders closeout sale.<br/><br/>I'm glad I did.<br/><br/>The book is a different experience than the movie. While both are dark and violent, the book is more passionate. It's a love letter of sorts, but filled with pain and loss. The author spends as much time coming to terms with the fact of having a loved one ripped from his life as he does imagining bloody revenge on th</span>
  <span id="freeText5390686309664810156" style="display:none">Though I saw (and loved) the film the year it came out, I had never read the original graphic novel until a few days ago, after buying it at the Borders closeout sale.<br /><br />I'm glad I did.<br /><br />The book is a different experience than the movie. While both are dark and violent, the book is more passionate. It's a love letter of sorts, but filled with pain and loss. The author spends as much time coming to terms with the fact of having a loved one ripped from his life as he does imagining bloody revenge on those who caused it. And the book is better for it.<br /><br />While some reviewers have decried the simplistic storyline and lack of development of villains and peripheral characters, I think these rather miss the point. The story isn't about these characters. Eric isn't on a quest of absolution and forgiveness; he doesn't (and by extension, we don't) really give a damn about who his murderers are as people, or even why they did what they did.<br /><br />As O'Barr deftly illustrates, revenge isn't about those upon whom we seek revenge. It isn't about current and future victims. Honestly, it isn't even necessarily about the victim for whom we are seeking vengeance. It's about us. Eric's revenge -- and the story itself -- is about Eric. Eric's rampage is itself a love letter written in blood to his dead fiance, and to their life together -- but ultimately it's a letter about his own pain, and his own loss.<br /><br />At the same time, though, there are those touches of humanity -- not only in Eric, but even in the villains. While they have no real backstory (and again, who needs it?), those upon whom Eric is seeking revenge do often start to come to grips with their own evil. They never apologize for it, but they recognize it. They don't seek -- and Eric doesn't offer -- forgiveness; instead they respond either with rage and indignation or else quiet acceptance. <br /><br />There are also small touches of realism in the story, which serve to make it that much more poignant. For me, it's often the little things which make or break a story, and O'Barr's attention to minor details really served the story here. One such detail was during the killing of Tom Tom. During the fight between Eric and Tom Tom, Eric used his sword to literally slice the feet out from under his enemy. As Eric gently grills him for information, Tom Tom looks over and says, "those are my boots," failing, in his shock, to realize that his feet are still in them. Minutes later, he asks Eric in an almost childlike way, "my feet are cold. Can I have my boots?" It's almost funny, and a lesser writer would have played the moment for laughs (and, I suppose, some readers will read it that way anyway). In O'Barr's hands, however, the moment is deadpanned and morose. It's not a joke at the villain's expense, but a simple fact of life: this is what shock is.<br /><br />There are going to be readers who are rather hidebound, and will rail against the non-traditional story structure and the lack of character development for all but the main character. And even with Eric, those same readers will likely complain about the complete lack of character arc. But as I said up top, it's simply not that kind of story. Eric is dead. And, as he explains more than once, those who killed Shelly are also dead already. The story isn't about those characters... it's about Eric's loss, and what he does about it. If he makes a friend or two along the way, those brief glimpses, too, are not about those characters, but about Eric.<br /><br />The Detroit of "The Crow" is a wasteland of crime and corruption. Ultimately, Eric's rampage isn't going to change anything for anyone. He's not setting out to be a superhero and rid the city of the festering rot that clings to its very soul. He's not going to make Cpt. Hook's life easier, or get Albrect a promotion. If later glimpses into her life tell us anything, not even Sherri -- the one living person with whom Eric has a nonviolent, semi-personal relationship -- will have her life dramatically improved by his brief entry into it. This is why, I believe, these other characters aren't more fully developed: they are more landscape than they are actual characters. They don't change Eric and, ultimately, he has little impact on them. It's both a realistic choice on the part of O'Barr and non-realistic. Sometimes, we change peoples' lives; sometimes we do not. O'Barr's depiction here is bleak, but not without accuracy.<br /><br />Don't look for a pleasing conclusion here; for answers on life and death. That's not what "The Crow" is. Instead, "The Crow" is a brooding rumination on loss, and on pain, and on love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5390686309664810156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_13540653" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13540653" />
    <a title="Robert Beveridge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/766524-robert-beveridge"><img alt="Robert Beveridge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286387835p2/766524.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13540653?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Beveridge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Beveridge" href="/user/show/766524-robert-beveridge">Robert Beveridge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/766524-robert-beveridge?shelf=finished">finished</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/766524-robert-beveridge?shelf=cuy-co-pub-lib">cuy-co-pub-lib</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13540653" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5192153692750339047">James O'Barr, The Crow (Pocket, 1995)<br/><br/>I always seem to be trying to turn sacred cows into shish kabob, and my latest target is The Crow, the James O'Barr graphic novel that, in 1994, became Alex Proyas' amazing debut feature film. I spent a few moments, after I finished this book, reflecting on the art of film adaptation. Sometimes you start with a brilliant book and end up with a mediocre (or outright horrible) movie. Sometimes you start with a mediocre (or outright horrible) book and end up wit</span>
  <span id="freeText5192153692750339047" style="display:none">James O'Barr, The Crow (Pocket, 1995)<br /><br />I always seem to be trying to turn sacred cows into shish kabob, and my latest target is The Crow, the James O'Barr graphic novel that, in 1994, became Alex Proyas' amazing debut feature film. I spent a few moments, after I finished this book, reflecting on the art of film adaptation. Sometimes you start with a brilliant book and end up with a mediocre (or outright horrible) movie. Sometimes you start with a mediocre (or outright horrible) book and end up with genius.<br /><br />The Crow is not an outright horrible book, but the steps that horror novelists David J. Schow (The Kill Riff) and John Shirley (Wetbones) took in adapting the book to a script, and that Proyas took in bringing that script to life, were pretty severe. Pretty amazing, too. Small details were changed (the order in which Eric takes his revenge is slightly altered, for example, to make it more meaningful); large details were changed (the timeframe makes a whole lot more sense in the film). Some of them were missteps; changing Sherry's name to Sarah muddies the Sherry/Shelly link, but Sarah is a fully-formed character, whereas Sherry feels like a character who should be there a lot more, but ends up being window dressing. <br /><br />The movie, on occasion, errs on the side of giving us too much, but the book's problem is the opposite: it never gives us enough. None of the characters except Eric is fully formed; his nemeses, with the exception of Funboy, are cardboard cutouts. Albrecht pops up for a few frames now and then, but never becomes a real part of the story. Etc.<br /><br />The movie is on its way to attaining classic status, and that is well-deserved; the book went out of print for eight years after the film's release, and now I've got a pretty good idea of why. **</span>
  <a data-text-id="5192153692750339047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2273509663267211495">Having seen the 1994 cult classic starring the late Brandon Lee in his last role, I wanted to check out the source material of <i>The Crow</i>, written and drawn by James O'Barr who has stated the origins of what is his very first book is based on a tragedy from his life.<br/><br/>With its hard boiled revenge story and black-and-white illustrations of bloody violence, at its heart the book is really a tragic love story of Eric longing to be reunited with the love of his life, whilst hunting down their killers wi</span>
  <span id="freeText2273509663267211495" style="display:none">Having seen the 1994 cult classic starring the late Brandon Lee in his last role, I wanted to check out the source material of <i>The Crow</i>, written and drawn by James O'Barr who has stated the origins of what is his very first book is based on a tragedy from his life.<br /><br />With its hard boiled revenge story and black-and-white illustrations of bloody violence, at its heart the book is really a tragic love story of Eric longing to be reunited with the love of his life, whilst hunting down their killers with the aid of a supernatural crow.<br /><br />O'Barr's artwork (of which is, in the author's words, influenced by Will Eisner) is extraordinary as it's not only gritty in regards with most pages, you get these poetic images of Eric with his fiancee Shelly.<br /><br />A truly violent, but moving tale of an avenging angel trying to regain his true love and in over twenty years, it's still a striking indie comic by James O'Barr.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3473931579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3473931579" />
    <a title="Michael Sorbello" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4307906-michael-sorbello"><img alt="Michael Sorbello" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521124600p2/4307906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3473931579?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Sorbello" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Sorbello" href="/user/show/4307906-michael-sorbello">Michael Sorbello</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780878162215" href="/book/show/603250.The_Crow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4307906-michael-sorbello?shelf=all-comics">all-comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4307906-michael-sorbello?shelf=book-reviews">book-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4307906-michael-sorbello?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3473931579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5380759925135440759">The story takes place in an alternate reality that looks something like an even more extreme, grim and merciless version of Batman’s Arkham Asylum. Despite the cruel and unforgiving society they live in, Eric Draven and his fiancé Shelly fall madly in love and find comfort in each other’s arms. Eric and Shelly go for a peaceful, romantic drive down the open road one fine day, enjoying each other’s company when their car is suddenly pulled over by a violent gang of thugs. They drag the two out of</span>
  <span id="freeText5380759925135440759" style="display:none">The story takes place in an alternate reality that looks something like an even more extreme, grim and merciless version of Batman’s Arkham Asylum. Despite the cruel and unforgiving society they live in, Eric Draven and his fiancé Shelly fall madly in love and find comfort in each other’s arms. Eric and Shelly go for a peaceful, romantic drive down the open road one fine day, enjoying each other’s company when their car is suddenly pulled over by a violent gang of thugs. They drag the two out of the car and they force Eric to watch them torture his girlfriend to death before shooting him in the back of the head. Just like that, the two lovers are dead. On the anniversary of their deaths, Eric resurrects from the grave as a vengeful spirit and takes on the mantle of a supernatural avenger, tracking down the thugs that killed him and his bride-to-be one by one and slaughtering them without mercy.<br /><br />The story begins with Eric violently interrogating a man named Mr. Jones, a thug with connections to the criminals that murdered him and Shelly. Here we get a taste of the protagonist’s cruelty and lust for vengeance. He’s not the sweet and compassionate man he used to be. He’s cruel, sadistic and enjoys the suffering of the people that wronged him. He’s no hero of justice and he doesn’t quite fit the definition of vigilante hero either, he’s just a heartbroken man fixated on personal revenge and he’s willing to throw away his humanity to get what he wants. The Crow does a great job of showing just how tragic and depressing revenge truly is. It’s not glorified or beautiful, but it can be dangerously satisfying, and that addiction to the pain can destroy a person from the inside.<br /><br />That’s the gist of the plot. A man resurrects from the dead and hunts down the men that killed him and his girlfriend. It’s a very simplistic revenge story with a unique gothic-horror aesthetic. Eric often goes into insane ramblings where he randomly babbles out dark poetry and edgy quotes like Edgar Allan Poe hopped up on drugs, vengeance and a rockstar charisma. Speaking of Poe, this story is pretty much what you would get if John Wick was written by Edgar Allan Poe. The villains are fairly one-dimensional, but it suits the mood of the story. This is about a broken man lashing out at the world because he's unable to contain his grief. It captures how one truly feels toward people that steal everything that was once precious away from you. There's no mercy or sympathy, there’s no attempt to see eye to eye or try to understand why they did what they did, there’s only a spiral of despair, rage and a self-consuming lust to give them the punishment they deserve<br /><br />The story feels more personal and intimate when you read it knowing that it’s loosely based on the author’s own experience and feelings of losing his significant other. You can feel his rage and despair pouring out from the writing and the bleak black and white pages smothered with rain, blood and grime. It also has an extremely good movie adaption featuring the late Brandon Lee, the son of Bruce Lee. It’s rich with a gothic atmosphere and has that nostalgic 1980’s action hero that throws out cool one liners vibe. I heard it was recently released on Netflix and I would definitely recommend giving it a watch.<br /><br />The original comic is a short and sweet self contained story, but there are quite a few spinoff stories where James collaborated with other authors to show off the dark avenger in action once more. In most of these stories, Eric resurrects other unfortunate souls that died tragic deaths and gives them the opportunity to avenge themselves like he once did. None of these stories are quite as good as the original in my opinion, but I thought they were worth mentioning for people that really want to see more from this cool character.<br /><br />***<br /><br />My Social Media<br /><br />My YouTube Channel: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCPPs1eOzsOqcz5ey5JjGSbg">https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCPPs...</a><br /><br />My Instagram Account: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.instagram.com/michael_sorbello/">https://www.instagram.com/michael_sor...</a><br /><br />My Wattpad Account: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.wattpad.com/user/Michael-Sorbello">https://www.wattpad.com/user/Michael-...</a><br /><br />My Twitter Account: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://twitter.com/SorbelloHorror">https://twitter.com/SorbelloHorror</a><br /><br />My Facebook Account: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/michael.sorbello.104">https://www.facebook.com/michael.sorb...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5380759925135440759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2214762735" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2214762735" />
    <a title="Ill D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5925555-ill-d"><img alt="Ill D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586395253p2/5925555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2214762735?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ill D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ill D" href="/user/show/5925555-ill-d">Ill D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5925555-ill-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5925555-ill-d?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5925555-ill-d?shelf=comic-reviews">comic-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2214762735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4401811653877381625"><br/>My feelings on The Crow are more mixed than a child's candy bag on Halloween.<br/><br/>There's good, not so good, and lots of stuff, that no matter how unique or evocative, doesn't necessarily hold up to snuff some thirty years later.<br/><br/>First of all, the influence of anime/manga (I remember when it was still colloquially referred to as "Japanamation" back in the day) is as egregious it is obvious. While this might be delightful to some, the sheer novelty of the influences might be off putting to others. It i</span>
  <span id="freeText4401811653877381625" style="display:none"><br />My feelings on The Crow are more mixed than a child's candy bag on Halloween.<br /><br />There's good, not so good, and lots of stuff, that no matter how unique or evocative, doesn't necessarily hold up to snuff some thirty years later.<br /><br />First of all, the influence of anime/manga (I remember when it was still colloquially referred to as "Japanamation" back in the day) is as egregious it is obvious. While this might be delightful to some, the sheer novelty of the influences might be off putting to others. It it might be lackluster to even further others now that Anime has enjoyed phenomenal success this side of the Pond for decades on end. <br /><br />Going along with this theme, Eric Draven the eponymous Crow, appears as if a group of Japan X fans, in full make-up, were tossed in a blender with a group of birthday clowns, and then denuded of all colors except for black and white. Again, while this very well might be cool as shit to many, for the no-so-anime fans such as myself (even though I'll happily admit I enjoy and have a great respect for innumerable animes that have enriched my life, DBZ, Gundamn Wing, Outlaw Star, etc...) it might seem a little off-putting at best. And at worst, it may not hold-up quite so well currently.<br /><br />While appearances of the protagonist are admittedly hit-and-miss the art style is definitely not. No matter your opinion, only the most downright cynical individual could find any fault with the phenomenal pencil-work of this series. As evocative as it is beautiful, the art style remains as beautiful as unique as it was thirty some years ago. Stellar detail pushes along a story as gritty and sublime as the words that underlay it. A doff of the cap here is not enough and O'Barr deserves nothing short of the highest praise for not just such beauteous works of art but their sheer uniqueness in the otherwise typically inky word of comics.<br /><br />Next, although the art-work is nothing short of stellar, it can as it does more often then not, dip into indulgence. Again, maybe some will enjoy The Crow performing innumerable pirouettes in the book (really). And maybe some will enjoy the numerous nostalgic romantic reminisces between the love interest. However, for the sake of conciseness/story development I felt this could have been tightened up a bit.<br /><br />While the tightness of the narrative is definitely up for debate/discussion the biggest fault I have with the story is the nature of the Crow's power. **SPOILER** Not all too different from Superman, on the other sides of the comic book galaxy, The Crow's main power is utter invulnerability. On a personal level I hate this power just as much as I truly loathe Superman. If the protagonist can't be hurt, let alone weakened, the drama of the battles is minimal at best. Eric shrugs off bullets just as well as the red and blue colored DC Übermench does. With no downside to the fights themselves the outcomes are disappointingly predictable. <br /><br />In either case, no matter its faults, The Crow is definitely a superb work. Born of real depression and existential angst, James O'Barr was able sublimate his pain and suffering into a piece of remarkable art with an equally awesome narrative to underlay it. <br /><br />With strong character development, a great narrative, and some of the prettiest art to ever grace a graphic novel James O'Barr's The Crow is highly recommended for comic book fans and readers alike.<br /><br />(Final Note, please read it in one session. It deserves your attention as such. You will thank me.)<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4401811653877381625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2274325412" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2274325412" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6822586-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545866767p2/6822586.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2274325412?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/6822586-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/593178.The_Crow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6822586-dan?shelf=2018-reading-challenge">2018-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6822586-dan?shelf=comic-books-graphic-novels">comic-books-graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6822586-dan?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2274325412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15382393347680792755">Well, I've been meaning to read this book for a long time now...I finally got around to doing it. <br/><br/>Yeah, the movie was something I grew up with and was very fond of, and I still am. I remember watching it over and over again, I even had a <em>The Crow</em> movie poster hanging on my bedroom wall. It was one of my favorite movies growing up and I still appreciate it. <br/><br/>I was happy to see that the first page was dedicated to the memory of <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brandon_Lee' rel='nofollow noopener'>Brandon Lee</a>. <br/><br/>Little differences here and there between the movie and th</span>
  <span id="freeText15382393347680792755" style="display:none">Well, I've been meaning to read this book for a long time now...I finally got around to doing it. <br /><br />Yeah, the movie was something I grew up with and was very fond of, and I still am. I remember watching it over and over again, I even had a <em>The Crow</em> movie poster hanging on my bedroom wall. It was one of my favorite movies growing up and I still appreciate it. <br /><br />I was happy to see that the first page was dedicated to the memory of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brandon_Lee" rel="nofollow noopener">Brandon Lee</a>. <br /><br />Little differences here and there between the movie and the book. Same concept, a guy named Eric and his fiance are murdered by a group of thugs and a crow brings him back from the dead to get revenge. Yet <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[there is no Devil's Night <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, that was added to the movie based on something that actually happened on Halloween in Detroit during the nineteen-seventies. And the young skateboarding girl in the movie, Sarah <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[her character is a kid named Sherri in the book <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Also, she's not nearly as interesting. The one thing I did not like is how O'Barr had Eric <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[steal Funboy's Morphine and use it himself to get high. He also cuts himself <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Movie Eric would never do that. <br /><br />There was a lot of poetry and song lyrics put into the story, so much that the main character (The Crow/Eric Draven) often quotes poems and speaks in riddles. <br /><br />I feel like the movie is a much more simplified version of the book. Not as many layers to cut through. <br /><br />From what I understand, from watching the special edition DVD...this was supposed to be a form of therapy for James O'Barr because his finance had been killed by a drunk driver and it took him ten years to make this thing. I like his work a lot. He has an early nineties underground comic book style. Very dark and gritty. Also somewhat gothic like the movie. I also like the fact that it's in black and white, I like to look at comic book art in black and white from time to time. I also noticed that a lot of the flashbacks in the story which were done mostly in inkwash or just pencil, were more realistic when the rest of the story was slightly more cartoony. <br /><br />So I enjoyed this book very much. The movie is currently being remade and James O'Barr apparently is involved in it. I hate the idea of a remake of <em>The Crow</em>, but if J. O'Barr is okay with it then to each their own I guess.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15382393347680792755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2768554164" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2768554164" />
    <a title="Nils | nilsreviewsit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52846007-nils-nilsreviewsit"><img alt="Nils | nilsreviewsit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560928352p2/52846007.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2768554164?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nils | nilsreviewsit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nils | nilsreviewsit" href="/user/show/52846007-nils-nilsreviewsit">Nils | nilsreviewsit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857687951" href="/book/show/12452215-the-crow">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2768554164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17266886751234609315">‘Who ever fights monsters should see to it that in the process he does not become a monster.’<br/>~<br/>The Crow by James O’Barr is the supernatural story centring around the past life of Eric and his fiancée, Shelly, and the tragic event that befalls them both. It’s a poignant and brutal story about not only vengeance and violence, but also about coping with grief and learning to forgive yourself for incidents that are out of your control.<br/><br/>Having watched the film prior to reading the graphic novel, I foun</span>
  <span id="freeText17266886751234609315" style="display:none">‘Who ever fights monsters should see to it that in the process he does not become a monster.’<br />~<br />The Crow by James O’Barr is the supernatural story centring around the past life of Eric and his fiancée, Shelly, and the tragic event that befalls them both. It’s a poignant and brutal story about not only vengeance and violence, but also about coping with grief and learning to forgive yourself for incidents that are out of your control.<br /><br />Having watched the film prior to reading the graphic novel, I found was in fact really helpful. I already knew the basic narrative, I knew what tragedy was coming, even though that event happens differently in the film, as to the graphic novel. It somehow made me feel much more of an emotional connection; I felt the pain and despair of Eric/the Crow that much deeper.<br /><br />The Crow was a very personal project for O’Barr. It was created after the author experienced a personal tragedy, and he spiralled into a dark place within himself; he created this graphic novel as a means of release, and it is all the more powerful for it.<br /><br />The illustrations were goddamn beautiful. They depicted every event so clearly, each scene had so many details. They even showed the striking contrast between the happier past and the darker present - scenes of the past with Eric and his fiancé, Shelly, were presented in a white background, whereas the parts where Eric is the Crow and is on his revenge quest had a black background. O’Barr had hand drawn all of this himself, which I found impressive.<br /><br />This is one somber read, O’Barr doesn’t hold back on how vile some humans can be, he expresses justice through bloodshed. You could really tell the author poured his heart and sole into this graphic novel, and that’s what truly captivated me and made this so heartbreaking.<br />~<br />‘Are the bones of your sins sharp enough to cut through your own excuses?’ </span>
  <a data-text-id="17266886751234609315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3216562343134013130" style="display:none">[10/4/19 just read this again while listening to NIN's The Downward Spiral and actually accidentally timed it out PERFECTLY to finish both at the same time. 11/10 would recommend new level of edgy depressed 90s bullshit lol]<br /><br />Every year around Halloween I reread this comic [and rewatch the movie] and every year I just sit around for weeks afterward trying to write a review that will actually do it justice but alas I am just not that great with words. It is just one of the most haunting, heart-breaking, and atmospheric graphic novels I've read and I definitely recommend it. <br /><br />By all rights it should read as slightly ridiculous. I mean on the surface it's about a guy in goth makeup on a revenge murder spree interspersed with random poetry, lyrics from The Smiths, and quotes like <i>There is a man, playing a violin, and the strings are the nerves in his own arm.</i> But it all comes together so beautifully that it just works.<br /><br />And if you're like me and automatically give most things a hard pass if they feature 'dead wife as plot device', maybe give this one a try because it's really not like all the other ones. You can tell that O'Barr's writing comes from a place of genuine pain and not just 'well i don't know what else to do with women in stories so might as well rape and kill them' like you see from a lot of authors who use this premise.</span>

    <a title="Bill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2887025-bill"><img alt="Bill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1256761633p2/2887025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill" href="/user/show/2887025-bill">Bill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2887025-bill?shelf=comic-books">comic-books</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76107327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6263400542560938303">My rating on this is more based on how it affected me when I first read it than how I'd necessarily react to it now. At 14, it was an absolute revelation - beyond all the dark, gloomy gothy trappings that aesthetically attracted me at that age, the emotion behind the creation of this is so raw and visceral as to be squirm inducing, which I'm sure also lined up with me at that age. <br/><br/>To be sure, there are some extremely problematic elements to the book, reading it with the benefit of age and experi</span>
  <span id="freeText6263400542560938303" style="display:none">My rating on this is more based on how it affected me when I first read it than how I'd necessarily react to it now. At 14, it was an absolute revelation - beyond all the dark, gloomy gothy trappings that aesthetically attracted me at that age, the emotion behind the creation of this is so raw and visceral as to be squirm inducing, which I'm sure also lined up with me at that age. <br /><br />To be sure, there are some extremely problematic elements to the book, reading it with the benefit of age and experience - the depictions of black characters in particular occasionally stray beyond gritty verisimilitude into the realm of racist caricature - but one thing that stands out in sharp relief to the other "revenge" comics of that time period is the overriding sense of the consequences and monstrosity of violence. Eric undoubtedly becomes something monstrous in order to take revenge on the people who violated he and his fiancee, and there is a sense at the end that maybe he can't come back from that, that by allowing himself to be brought back to take revenge, he's lost the person he loves completely, which is a much more complex, nuanced approach to the "revenge fantasy" than most comics at the time were able to pull off. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6263400542560938303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60595464-miss?shelf=love-at-first-smell">love-at-first-smell</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2240755152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6548562954854815926">Where the hell disappeared my review? I could swear it wasn‘t accidentally deleted, uh, now I need to share the love for this work again. At least, the precious time won‘t be wasted on something else.<br/><br/>Talking about adaptations, there are two types of people and I belong to those who almost always watch the movies first, then read the books. If not the Nolan‘s <i>"Batman" </i>trilogy, <i>"The Killing Joke"</i> comics would still be unknown to me, if not the adaptation starring Brandon Lee, the brilliant O‘Barr </span>
  <span id="freeText6548562954854815926" style="display:none">Where the hell disappeared my review? I could swear it wasn‘t accidentally deleted, uh, now I need to share the love for this work again. At least, the precious time won‘t be wasted on something else.<br><br>Talking about adaptations, there are two types of people and I belong to those who almost always watch the movies first, then read the books. If not the Nolan‘s <i>"Batman" </i>trilogy, <i>"The Killing Joke"</i> comics would still be unknown to me, if not the adaptation starring Brandon Lee, the brilliant O‘Barr <i>"The Crow"</i> wouldn‘t be in my „would read again“ shelf. I can proudly say I am a big fan of both, the mentioned movies and the books.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Introduction &amp; "Pain". </u>
  </i>
</b><br><i>„One day you are going to lose everything you have. Nothing will prepare you for that day. Not faith... Not religion... Nothing.“</i> – what a beautiful way to start a novel. The most I liked the fact that James O‘Barr took inspiration from various places: his own experience, real-life events, music. It creates a very realistic dark atmosphere together with bands-influencers: The Cure, Joy Division. I could have listened to them to make a better effect, but instead, I chose Evanescence‘s old albums, and Amy Lee's voice fitted the comics very well too. Surrounded by great music in the background Eric and his crow soon started the journey. <br><br><b>Favourite moment (was in the novel too):</b><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515183781ra/24838312.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515183781ra/24838313.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515183781ra/24838314.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>"Fear".</u>
  </i>
</b><br>The Joker Ledger created obviously shared many similarities with Eric Draven. An interesting thing I noticed is the connection between Joker's quote <i>"Madness, as you know, is like gravity. All it takes is a little push."</i> and the episode where Draven says <i>"I am complete and total madness".</i> When you think better, everything seems logical. If shocking Shelly's death is that little push to the edge, then Eric is truly an example of madness.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>"Despair".</u>
  </i>
</b><br>He calls her 'ballerina', she calls him 'handsome'.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1515184552i/24838374._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>"Death".</u>
  </i>
</b><br>An adorable moment with a pregnant Gabrielle (re-named). Hook will totally love those kittens.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1515184552i/24838375._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I could continue talking for hours, but let's stop here. <i>"The Crow"</i> melted my heart as it was a piece of ice. Eric story ended, but he still lives in my head, I still can hear his voice. He won't leave. Ever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6548562954854815926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1637304202" />
    <a title="Michael Benavidez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25042657-michael-benavidez"><img alt="Michael Benavidez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595810258p2/25042657.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1637304202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15916641857078012402">This intro, is probably the most heart wrenching intro I think I've ever. It talks about what the Crow really was about, and the story behind it, and you can just feel the emotional weight within the words. It's so heavy, and it adds a full perspective to the graphic novel that follows. <br/>The graphic novel itself is far darker than the movie, though the legacy of the movie is a dark one, and they both tap into something different. The graphic novel goes for creating the main character as someone w</span>
  <span id="freeText15916641857078012402" style="display:none">This intro, is probably the most heart wrenching intro I think I've ever. It talks about what the Crow really was about, and the story behind it, and you can just feel the emotional weight within the words. It's so heavy, and it adds a full perspective to the graphic novel that follows. <br />The graphic novel itself is far darker than the movie, though the legacy of the movie is a dark one, and they both tap into something different. The graphic novel goes for creating the main character as someone we understand, and what we kinda wish we could do to our feelings of guilt and the like. The bad guys though are just cannon fodder, but that's what they should be. This isn't a story about them, it's a story about dealing with the guilt and grief and turning into a monster and dealing with it. <br />The thing that caught me though, was the language. One moment it's poetic verses, that detail exactly what's going on in the minds of the character, to the grit of slang used by these assholes who we just want killed.<br />Reading it with the heavy weight of what brought this story to life, there's an even bigger emotional pull, all the words, verses, and malice just hang heavier. I'm still kinda reeling from it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15916641857078012402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1060211040" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1060211040" />
    <a title="Quentin Wallace" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33274222-quentin-wallace"><img alt="Quentin Wallace" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595984858p2/33274222.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1060211040?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quentin Wallace" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quentin Wallace" href="/user/show/33274222-quentin-wallace">Quentin Wallace</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1060211040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17921266572108484685">The first and probably the best of the Crow comics. The art is great, very moody and gritty in black and white. The storyline is good as well, but sometimes tends to meander and get a little existential. The movie did a good job of bringing the story to its core, which is a revenge story where The Crow comes back from the dead and avenges the death of his fiancee and himself. I love the mythology of the Crow bringing the spirit back from the dead to take care of unfinished business as well. The </span>
  <span id="freeText17921266572108484685" style="display:none">The first and probably the best of the Crow comics. The art is great, very moody and gritty in black and white. The storyline is good as well, but sometimes tends to meander and get a little existential. The movie did a good job of bringing the story to its core, which is a revenge story where The Crow comes back from the dead and avenges the death of his fiancee and himself. I love the mythology of the Crow bringing the spirit back from the dead to take care of unfinished business as well. The story from the movie is in this comic, but the comic isn't as straight forward as the movie. However, the comic is so well done and the art is so good you don't mind seeing some art just for art's sake. This comic is as much gothic literature as it is a graphic novel. <br /><br />If you are a fan of all things gothic, or comics in general, I would recommend this to you. As far as non comic fans go, this could be a little weird to you, or you could love it as well, it's hard to say. If you liked the movie, you'd probably like the comic. And even if you hated the movie, but love dark and moody art, you'd probably like this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17921266572108484685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14194254857566997985">I gotta admit it, I am a bit disappointed with this graphic novel. The crow is famous within the cult group of comics and movies, but, for some reason, I was expecting more.<br/>O' Barr's novel tells the story of Eric, who returns from the dead. Blinded by hate, vengeance and anger, the Protagonist seeks vengeance on those who killed him and his beloved dame, Shelly.<br/>The story is in its entirely a witness of anger, and loathing, with violent combat scenes.   <span id="freeText14194254857566997985" style="display:none">I gotta admit it, I am a bit disappointed with this graphic novel. The crow is famous within the cult group of comics and movies, but, for some reason, I was expecting more.<br />O' Barr's novel tells the story of Eric, who returns from the dead. Blinded by hate, vengeance and anger, the Protagonist seeks vengeance on those who killed him and his beloved dame, Shelly.<br />The story is in its entirely a witness of anger, and loathing, with violent combat scenes. The Crow is undoubtedly dark, and the author managed to emphasize Eric's pain, but, at times it started to become repeatedly annoying. In addition, I was expecting to see more development within the villains characters. <br /><br />Overall, I found the artwork quite raw, attracting with bold shady lines. The general concept, initially, is interesting, but, as the story progresses, it starts to be a bit boring.<br />I give three starts for the artwork, the general story's concept, and the witty narrative by Eric.<br /><br />...perhaps it deserves a second opportunity in the future.<br /><br />Rating: 3/5 stars</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Nelson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Nelson" href="/user/show/7553206-paul-nelson">Paul Nelson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer641608014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10161728574128429831">The movie, The Crow came out in 1994 and I remember being blown away by the performance of Brandon Lee who tragically died during filming and its a movie I will never tire of, watching it again and again every few years when the mood takes me.<br/><br/>A grief stricken tale of revenge this is as dark and brutal as it gets. Eric and the love of his life Shelley are murdered on a rural highway next to their broken down car. A year to the day, Eric is bought back, fuelled by rage and guided by the Crow he se</span>
  <span id="freeText10161728574128429831" style="display:none">The movie, The Crow came out in 1994 and I remember being blown away by the performance of Brandon Lee who tragically died during filming and its a movie I will never tire of, watching it again and again every few years when the mood takes me.<br /><br />A grief stricken tale of revenge this is as dark and brutal as it gets. Eric and the love of his life Shelley are murdered on a rural highway next to their broken down car. A year to the day, Eric is bought back, fuelled by rage and guided by the Crow he seeks retribution on the gang members that blew his love apart. <br />The story opens with the start of Eric's search and we see the intense love of the couple through flashbacks, the sorrow and despair of a lost love are overwhelming and a stark contrast to the violence and the gothic tone of the book.<br />The artwork is dark and moody, vividly bringing the character and his desires to life, the overall feeling of sorrow outweighs the tale of vengeance, heightened by the use of almost sermon type monologue as he ends the lives of the killers. A passionate tale that stirs the emotions, as poetic as it is dark but at its core, its a love story and a terrible tragedy.<br /><br />The novel is packed out with additional images of the lovers and also poetry and song lyrics that I guess resonate the tone of the story, not really my type of thing but it will appeal to some. The story itself was enough for me and its something that left a big impression on me in my younger days and still does to this day.<br /><br />The first instalment of this novel was released in 1988, echoing the tragedy of the writers own despair at the loss of his girlfriend to a drunk driver.<br />In conclusion this is a poignant and haunting tale, one that will stay with me probably for the rest of my days... <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10161728574128429831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_496080182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/496080182" />
    <a title="Efrain Martinez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15341605-efrain-martinez"><img alt="Efrain Martinez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357366029p2/15341605.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/496080182?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Efrain Martinez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Efrain Martinez" href="/user/show/15341605-efrain-martinez">Efrain Martinez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer496080182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14521296507216512145">(Recommended Pandora station for this book: Wu-Tang Clan) If you're interested in reading this book don't pay attention to any review that compares it to the movie. The book should stand alone as a separate piece of work. Due to major plot changes and the creative motivation behind "The Crow". I read this when it first came out in 93'. I remember it blew my teenaged mind. My buddy and I couldn't wait for the next issue to come out! We were pulled in by the intensity of the story and the characte</span>
  <span id="freeText14521296507216512145" style="display:none">(Recommended Pandora station for this book: Wu-Tang Clan) If you're interested in reading this book don't pay attention to any review that compares it to the movie. The book should stand alone as a separate piece of work. Due to major plot changes and the creative motivation behind "The Crow". I read this when it first came out in 93'. I remember it blew my teenaged mind. My buddy and I couldn't wait for the next issue to come out! We were pulled in by the intensity of the story and the characters wants and motivations. What would you be willing to do if god sent you back as tool of revenge? At the time that it came out it took the concept of the anti-hero and mixed in some meth and vengeful violence. The movie changed the ending which was one of my favorite moments in the book. Also given the history and tragedy that brought the author to the creation of the book's concept creates a aggressively deeper meaning and impact to the overall story. What are you willing to do to the men who took the life of your love? At what level is revenge satisfied? A classic modern day comic read. If you like the book stay away from all other "Crow" related stories and productions. They all pretty much suck and the majority of them did not involve the original creative talent behind the first collection. Read this book to you're inner teen and enjoy, I still do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14521296507216512145" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3388400045798891644">Two graphic novels in one night. Both about someone returning from the dead to take revenge on a rape and murder. wtf?! I pick up three books randomly and i seem to have stumbled across the dark side of this graphic world.<br/>This was just plan grim. Dark, dirty and just pure violence and tortured souls. THe author has a very very dark place he can call on to write this story, because there was nothing here at all to enjoy. <br/>I could imagine wallowing in the depths of dispair and becoming depressed re</span>
  <span id="freeText3388400045798891644" style="display:none">Two graphic novels in one night. Both about someone returning from the dead to take revenge on a rape and murder. wtf?! I pick up three books randomly and i seem to have stumbled across the dark side of this graphic world.<br />This was just plan grim. Dark, dirty and just pure violence and tortured souls. THe author has a very very dark place he can call on to write this story, because there was nothing here at all to enjoy. <br />I could imagine wallowing in the depths of dispair and becoming depressed reading too much of this stuff. I chose a couple fo graphics for a light break from the fantasy and scifi I have been reading and I find myself gloomy and sad.<br /><br />I understand all the hype surrounding the movie, but seriously, this is just violence with a small story wrapped around it. The characters are so freaking one dimensional and stereotypical it is ridiculous.<br /><br />S why two stars and not one? Its pretty powerful stuff, not my kind of stuff, but real heavy and I can see a lot of people liking its darkness. So two stars. <br /><br />lets hope the third random choice will be more entertaining and cheerful. </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1604400732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10352787303977866024">I haven't read this since middle school, but damn if it doesn't hold up. It's dark and gritty, and you can't stop reading it. It's a painful look at the life of a man whose one true love, is violently raped and murdered right in front of him (granted he's virtually dead so he can't exactly stop them). A year later he is somehow back, having rejected death, he goes out to seek revenge on the gang of thugs and addicts that ruined his life and exact his justice. Filled with lots of flashbacks, poem</span>
  <span id="freeText10352787303977866024" style="display:none">I haven't read this since middle school, but damn if it doesn't hold up. It's dark and gritty, and you can't stop reading it. It's a painful look at the life of a man whose one true love, is violently raped and murdered right in front of him (granted he's virtually dead so he can't exactly stop them). A year later he is somehow back, having rejected death, he goes out to seek revenge on the gang of thugs and addicts that ruined his life and exact his justice. Filled with lots of flashbacks, poems, and agony this graphic novel is not for the light hearted, but it is well worth a read and remains one of my personal favorites. The cult film based off of this, is also not to be missed. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10352787303977866024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2562421373" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16482366096250536255">I'm giving this three stars based sheerly on nostalgia. I read this in comic book form as a college student back in the late 80's when it first came out, and I remember thinking it was really cool. As an adult, I find it pretty unreadable goth garbage from a creator who was just getting started. This edition inserts new pages of art into the original story, and they really drive home how much O'Barr matured as an artist in the years following his big breakthrough.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54842999-angela-auten?shelf=nook-books">nook-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54842999-angela-auten?shelf=2-star-books">2-star-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1771192381" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16435532953611409908">The Crow Review <br/><br/>Comic Book Rating: 2 stars out of 5. <br/><br/>Story Line: 2 stars out of 5. I want to start off by saying that I really wanted to read the original version of the Crow comic. With that being said I am a little biased when it comes to this comic because I watched the movie first. This is one of the first times that I loved the movie more than book/comic version. <br/><br/>This comic is pretty dark. I like that it's dark, but some of the scenes didn't mesh up to the movie...I was really disappointed.</span>
  <span id="freeText16435532953611409908" style="display:none">The Crow Review <br /><br />Comic Book Rating: 2 stars out of 5. <br /><br />Story Line: 2 stars out of 5. I want to start off by saying that I really wanted to read the original version of the Crow comic. With that being said I am a little biased when it comes to this comic because I watched the movie first. This is one of the first times that I loved the movie more than book/comic version. <br /><br />This comic is pretty dark. I like that it's dark, but some of the scenes didn't mesh up to the movie...I was really disappointed. I guess because Brandon Lee portrayed the character very well in the movie. He brought him to life for me. The comic really didn't. I love when books or comics do that. It was harder for me to connect with Eric in the comic. This is actually pretty hard for me to review honestly because I didn't expect to not like it. The comic really didn't meet my expectations...<br /><br />One other thing that you should know is that I don't really like reading American comic books that much, but James O'Barr is a great artist. I prefer manga. I like the art style better for it. That may also be another reason that this comic didn't meet my expectations. <br /><br />I also feel horrible for giving this book this low of a rating. I'm just happy that I liked it just enough to give it two stars. I also will give it a pass since I really loved the movie. <br /><br />I also respect that James O'Barr gave Brandon Lee a tribute in the beginning of the book. What happened on the set of this movie was a pure tragedy. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16435532953611409908" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+Z91mJHK3oaxv5ivjcIyatbzXOClA9L0H7OoX9T6UonBRlTbDiwqIJQnpG/wJyK9Q9aRdupl9OR0Lo7pDJf/lA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Gorey tells the tale of 26 children (each representing a letter of the alphabet) and their untimely deaths in rhyming dactylic couplets, accompanied by the author\'s distinctive black and white illustrations. It is one of Edward Gorey\'s best-known books, and is the most notorious amongst his roughly half-dozen mock alphabets.[2] It has been described as a &quot;sarcastic rebellion against a view of childhood that is sunny, idyllic, and instructive&quot;. The morbid humor of the book comes in part from the mundane ways in which children die, such as falling down the stairs or choking on a peach. Far from illustrating the dramatic and fantastical childhood nightmares, these scenarios instead poke fun at the banal paranoias that come as a part of parenting.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/56002.James_O_Barr">About James O&#39;Barr</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/56002.James_O_Barr"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1343843816p3/56002.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/56002.James_O_Barr">James O'Barr
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17959904080272124267">James O'Barr is an accomplished artist and writer, best known for creating <i>The Crow</i>.<br/><br/>In 1978, O'Barr's fiancée, Beverly, was killed by a drunk driver, and he joined the Marines in an effort to cope with the loss. He was stationed in Germany and illustrated combat manuals for the military. While living in Berlin in 1981, O'Barr began work on The Crow as a means of dealing with his personal tragedy. </span>
  <span id="freeText17959904080272124267" style="display:none">James O'Barr is an accomplished artist and writer, best known for creating <i>The Crow</i>.<br /><br />In 1978, O'Barr's fiancée, Beverly, was killed by a drunk driver, and he joined the Marines in an effort to cope with the loss. He was stationed in Germany and illustrated combat manuals for the military. While living in Berlin in 1981, O'Barr began work on The Crow as a means of dealing with his personal tragedy. O'Barr was further inspired by a Detroit newspaper account of the murder of a young couple over a $20 engagement ring. After his discharge from the Marines, O'Barr continued his painting and illustration as well as doing lots of odd jobs, including working for a Detroit body shop. The Crow sat on a shelf for seven years, but at last someone wanted to publish it: Gary Reed of Caliber Press. In The Crow, the protagonist and his fiancée are murdered by a gang of criminals. He then returns from the dead to hunt their killers.<br /><br />O'Barr's own hope that his project would result in a personal catharsis went unfulfilled, he told an interviewer in 1994, saying, "[A]s I drew each page, it made me more self-destructive, if anything....There is pure anger on each page". The Crow has sold more than 750,000 copies worldwide.<br /><br />The book was adapted into a successful film of the same name in 1994, but it resulted in further tragedy. Brandon Lee, who played the main character, was accidentally shot and killed during filming.<br /><br />O'Barr was the second American to be awarded the "Storyteller Award" by the International Comic Festival held annually in Angoulême, France.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17959904080272124267" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
